   474	I. Introduction
   475	
   476	Table of Contents
   477	1. Introduction
   478	
   479	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
   480	
   481	Chapter 1. Introduction
   482	
   483	Introduction
   484	
   485	This is the Linux Standard Base Work In Progress 20030509 . An implementation
   486	of anything in this document may not claim to be an implementation of the Linux
   487	Standard Base until such time as the feature moves into the Linux Standards
   488	Base Specification.
   489	
   490	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
   491	
   492	Purpose
   493	
   494	The Linux Standard Base (LSB) defines a system interface for compiled
   495	applications and a minimal environment for support of installation scripts. Its
   496	purpose is to enable a uniform industry standard environment for high-volume
   497	applications conforming to the LSB.
   498	
   499	The LSB defines a binary interface for application programs that are compiled
   500	and packaged for LSB-conforming implementations on many different hardware
   501	architectures. Since a binary specification must include information specific
   502	to the computer processor architecture for which it is intended, it is not
   503	possible for a single document to specify the interface for all possible
   504	LSB-conforming implementations. Therefore, the LSB is a family of
   505	specifications, rather than a single one.
   506	
   507	The LSB is composed of two basic parts: A common specification ("generic LSB"
   508	or "gLSB") describing those parts of the interface that remain constant across
   509	all implementations of the LSB, and an architecture-specific specification
   510	("archLSB") describing the parts of the interface that vary by processor
   511	architecture. Together, the generic LSB and the architecture-specific
   512	supplement for a single hardware architecture provide a complete interface
   513	specification for compiled application programs on systems that share a common
   514	hardware architecture.
   515	
   516	This document is the generic LSB. It must be used in conjunction with an
   517	architecture-specific supplement. Whenever a section of this specification must
   518	be supplemented by architecture-specific information, this document includes a
   519	reference to the architecture supplement. Architecture supplements may also
   520	contain additional information that is not referenced here.
   521	
   522	This document should be used in conjunction with the documents it references.
   523	This document enumerates the system components it includes, but descriptions of
   524	those components may be included entirely or partly in this document, partly in
   525	other documents, or entirely in other reference documents. For example, the
   526	section that describes system service routines includes a list of the system
   527	routines supported in this interface, formal declarations of the data
   528	structures they use that are visible to applications, and a pointer to the
   529	underlying referenced specification for information about the syntax and
   530	semantics of each call. Only those routines not described in standards
   531	referenced by this document, or extensions to those standards, are described in
   532	the detail. Information referenced in this way is as much a part of this
   533	document as is the information explicitly included here.
   534	
   535	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
   536	
   537	Related Standards
   538	
   539	The specifications listed below are referenced in whole or in part by the Linux
   540	Standard Base. When a specification is referenced in a way that imposes a
   541	requirement (for example, "foo must behave as specified in the XyzzySpec"),
   542	then the relevant requirements of that specification apply as if they were part
   543	of the LSB itself. However, if the LSB refers to a specification without
   544	imposing a requirement, then it is merely a reference and does not add
   545	additional requirements. The LSB may refer to a portion of a specification (for
   546	example, to define a specific function or group of functions); in such cases,
   547	only the explicitly referenced portion of the specification applies.

   725	
   726	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
   727	
   728	How to Use this Standard
   729	
   730	The complete LSB specification is composed of this generic LSB specification
   731	and the supplemental processor-specific specification for a particular
   732	processor architecture. These two documents constitute a specification that
   733	should be used in conjunction with the publicly-available standards documents
   734	it references. The LSB enumerates the system components it includes, but
   735	descriptions of those components may be included entirely in the LSB, partly in
   736	the LSB and partly in other documents, or entirely in other reference
   737	documents.
   738	
   739	II. Base Libraries

   741	Table of Contents

   744	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------

   746	Chapter 2. Libraries
   747	
   748	An LSB-conforming implementation shall support some base libraries which
   749	provide interfaces for accessing the operating system, processor and other
   750	hardware in the system.

  8540	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
  8541	
  8542	Data Definitions for libstdcxx
  8543	
  8544	This section contains standard data definitions that describe system data.
  8545	These definitions are organized into groups that correspond to system headers.
  8546	This convention is used as a convenience for the reader, and does not imply the
  8547	existence of these headers, or their content.
  8548	
  8549	ISO C serves as the LSB reference programming language, and data definitions
  8550	are specified in ISO C format. The C language is used here as a convenient
  8551	notation. Using a C language description of these data objects does not
  8552	preclude their use by other programming languages.
  8553	
  8554	III. System Initialization
  8555	
  8556	Table of Contents
  8557	3. System Initialization
  8558	
  8559	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
  8560	
  8561	Chapter 3. System Initialization
  8562	
  8563	Cron Jobs
  8564	
  8565	Packages may not touch the configuration file /etc/crontab, nor may they modify
  8566	the files in /var/spool/cron/crontabs.
  8567	
  8568	If a package wants to install a job that has to be executed via cron, it shall
  8569	place a file in one of the following directories:
  8570	
  8571	/etc/cron.daily
  8572	/etc/cron.weekly
  8573	/etc/cron.monthly
  8574	
  8575	As these directory names say, the files within them are executed on a daily,
  8576	weekly, or monthly basis, respectively. See below for the rules concerning the
  8577	names of these files.
  8578	
  8579	 If a certain job has to be executed more frequently than daily, the package
  8580	shall install a file /etc/cron.d/cron-name tagged as configuration file. This
  8581	file uses the same syntax as /etc/crontab and is processed by cron
  8582	automatically.
  8583	
  8584	It is recommended that files installed in any of these directories be scripts
  8585	(shell scripts, Perl scripts, etc.) so that they may be modified by the local
  8586	system administrator. In addition, they must be registered as configuration
  8587	file.
  8588	
  8589	The scripts in these directories have to check, if all necessary programs are
  8590	installed before they try to execute them. Otherwise, problems will arise when
  8591	a package was removed (but not purged), since the configuration files are kept
  8592	on the system in this situation.
  8593	
  8594	To avoid namespace conflicts in the /etc/cron.* directories, the filenames used
  8595	by LSB-compliant packages in /etc/cron.daily, /etc/cron.weekly, /etc/
  8596	cron.monthly, or /etc/cron.d must come from a managed namespace. These
  8597	filenames may be assigned using one of the following methods:
  8598	
  8599	  * Assigned namespace. This namespace consists of names which only use the
  8600	    character set [a-z0-9]. In order to avoid conflicts these cron script names
  8601	    must be reserved through the Linux Assigned Names and Numbers Authority
  8602	    (LANANA). Information about the LANANA may be found at www.lanana.org.
  8603	   
  8604	    Commonly used names shall be reserved in advance; developers for projects
  8605	    should be encouraged reserve names from LANA, so that each distribution can
  8606	    use the same name, and to avoid conflicts with other projects.
  8607	   
  8608	  * Hierarchical namespace. This namespace consists of scripts names which look
  8609	    like this: [hier1]-[hier2]-...-[name], where name is again taken the
  8610	    character set [a-z0-9], and where there may be one or more [hier-n]
  8611	    components. [hier1] may either be an LSB provider name assigned by the
  8612	    LANANA, or it may be owners' DNS name in lower case, with at least one '.'.
  8613	    I.e., "debian.org", "staroffice.sun.com", etc. The LSB provider name
  8614	    assigned by LANANA must only consist of the ASCII characters [a-z0-9].
  8615	   
  8616	  * Reserved namespace. This namespace consists of script names which begin
  8617	    with the character '_', and is reserved for distribution use only. This
  8618	    namespace should be used for core packages only, and in general use of this
  8619	    namespace is highly discouraged.
  8620	   
  8621	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
  8622	
  8623	Init Script Actions
  8624	
  8625	Init files provided by LSB applications shall accept one argument, saying what
  8626	to do:
  8627	
  8628	start        start the service                                                 
  8629	stop         stop the service                                                  
  8630	restart      stop and restart the service if the service is already running,   
  8631	             otherwise start the service                                       
  8632	try-restart  restart the service if the service is already running             
  8633	reload       cause the configuration of the service to be reloaded without     
  8634	             actually stopping and restarting the service                      
  8635	force-reload cause the configuration to be reloaded if the service supports    
  8636	             this, otherwise restart the service                               
  8637	status       print the current status of the service                           
  8638	
  8639	The start, stop, restart, force-reload, and status commands must be supported
  8640	by all init files; the reload and the try-restart options are optional. Other
  8641	init script actions may be defined by the init script.
  8642	
  8643	Init files must ensure that they will behave sensibly if invoked with start
  8644	when the service is already running, or with stop when it isn't, and that they
  8645	don't kill unfortunately-named user processes. The best way to achieve this is
  8646	to use the init-script functions provided by /lib/lsb/init-functions.
  8647	
  8648	If a service reloads its configuration automatically (as in the case of cron,
  8649	for example), the reload option of the init file must behave as if the
  8650	configuration has been reloaded successfully.
  8651	
  8652	These executable files must not fail obscurely when the configuration files
  8653	remain but the package has been removed, as the default in [the packaging
  8654	system] is to leave configuration files on the system after the package has
  8655	been removed. Only when it is executed with the [purge] option will [the
  8656	packaging system] remove configuration files. Therefore, you should include a
  8657	test statement at the top of the file, like this:
  8658	
  8659	test -f program-executed-later-in-file || exit 5
  8660	
  8661	or take the equivalent action if the init file is not a shell script.
  8662	
  8663	If the status command is given, the init script will return the following exit
  8664	status codes.
  8665	
  8666	0       program is running or service is OK           
  8667	1       program is dead and /var/run pid file exists  
  8668	2       program is dead and /var/lock lock file exists
  8669	3       program is stopped                            
  8670	4       program or service status is unknown          
  8671	5-99    reserved for future LSB use                   
  8672	100-149 reserved for distribution use                 
  8673	150-199 reserved for application use                  
  8674	200-254 reserved                                      
  8675	
  8676	In the case of init script commands other than "status" (i.e., "start", "stop",
  8677	"restart", "reload", and "force-reload"), the init script must return an exit
  8678	status of zero if the action described by the argument has been successful.
  8679	Otherwise, the exit status shall be non-zero, as defined below. In addition to
  8680	straightforward success, the following situations are also to be considered
  8681	successful:
  8682	
  8683	  * restarting a service (instead of reloading it) with the "force-reload"
  8684	    argument
  8685	   
  8686	  * running "start" on a service already running
  8687	   
  8688	  * running "stop" on a service already stopped or not running
  8689	   
  8690	  * running "restart" on a service already stopped or not running
  8691	   
  8692	 
  8693	
  8694	In case of an error, while processing any init script action except for
  8695	"status", the init script must print an error message and return one of the
  8696	following non-zero exit status codes.
  8697	
  8698	1       generic or unspecified error (current practice)
  8699	2       invalid or excess argument(s)                  
  8700	3       unimplemented feature (for example, "reload")  
  8701	4       user had insufficient privilege                
  8702	5       program is not installed                       
  8703	6       program is not configured                      
  8704	7       program is not running                         
  8705	8-99    reserved for future LSB use                    
  8706	100-149 reserved for distribution use                  
  8707	150-199 reserved for application use                   
  8708	200-254 reserved                                       
  8709	
  8710	All error messages must be printed on standard error. All status messages must
  8711	be printed on standard output. (This does not prevent scripts from calling the
  8712	logging functions such as log_failure_msg).
  8713	
  8714	Since init files may be run manually by a system administrator with
  8715	non-standard environment variable values for PATH, USER, LOGNAME, etc. init
  8716	files must not depend on the values of these environment variables. They should
  8717	set them to some known/default values if they are needed. 
  8718	
  8719	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
  8720	
  8721	Comment conventions for init scripts
  8722	
  8723	LSB applications which need to execute script(s) at bootup and/or shutdown may
  8724	provide one or more init.d files. These files are installed by the
  8725	install_initd program described below, which copies it into a standard
  8726	directory and makes whatever other adjustments (creation of symlinks, creation
  8727	of entries in a database, etc.) are necessary so that the script can be run at
  8728	boot-time. [3]
  8729	
  8730	In the init.d file, information about the shell script shall be delimited by
  8731	the lines "### BEGIN INIT INFO" and "### END INIT INFO". These delimiter lines
  8732	may containg trailing whitespace, which shall be ignored. Inside this block
  8733	there shall be lines of the form "# {keyword}: [arg1] [arg2] ...". (All lines
  8734	inside this block start with a hash ('#') character in the first column, so
  8735	that shell treats them as comments.) There must be exactly one space character
  8736	between "#" and the keyword.[4] The following keywords, with their arguments
  8737	are defined in this specification:
  8738	
  8739	        # Provides: boot_facility_1 [ boot_facility_2 ...]
  8740	        # Required-Start: boot_facility_1 [ boot_facility_2 ...]
  8741	        # Required-Stop: boot_facility_1 [ boot_facility_2 ...]
  8742	        # Should-Start: boot_facility_1 [ boot_facility_2 ...]
  8743	        # Should-Stop: boot_facility_1 [ boot_facility_2 ...]
  8744	        # Default-Start: run_level_1 [ run_level_2 ...]
  8745	        # Default-Stop: run_level_1 [ run_level_2 ...]
  8746	        # Short-Description: short_description
  8747	        # Description: multiline_description
  8748	
  8749	Additional keywords may be defined in future LSB specifications. Distributions
  8750	may define local extensions by using the prefix "X-[distribution name]" --- for
  8751	example, "X-RedHat-foobardecl", or "X-Debian-xyzzydecl".
  8752	
  8753	An init.d shell script may declare using the "Required-Start: " header that it
  8754	must not be run until certain boot facilities are provided. This information is
  8755	used by the installation tool or the boot-time boot-script execution facility
  8756	to assure that init scripts are run in the correct order. When an init script
  8757	is run with a "start" argument, the boot facility or facilities specified in
  8758	the "Provides" header shall be considered present, and hence init scripts which
  8759	require those boot facilities would then be eligble to be run. When an init
  8760	script is run with a "stop" argument, the boot facilities specified in the
  8761	"Provides" header are considered no longer present. There are naming
  8762	conventions for boot facilities and system facilities, as described in a
  8763	following section.
  8764	
  8765	Similarly, the "Required-Stop:" header defines which facilities must still be
  8766	available during the shutdown of that service. Hence, the init script system
  8767	should avoid stopping shell scripts which provide those facilities until this
  8768	shell script is stopped.
  8769	
  8770	The "Should-Start:" header defines which facilities if present should be
  8771	started before this service. This allows for weak dependencies which do not
  8772	cause the service to fail if a facility is not available. But may cause reduced
  8773	functionality of the service. Compliant applications should not rely on the
  8774	existence of this feature.
  8775	
  8776	The "Should-Stop:" header defines which facilities should be still available
  8777	during the shutdown of that service.
  8778	
  8779	The "Default-Start" and "Default-Stop" headers define which run levels should
  8780	by default run the script with a start or stop argument, respectively, to start
  8781	or stop the services controlled by the init script.
  8782	
  8783	The "Short-Description" and "Description" header fields are used to provide
  8784	text which describes the actions of the init script. The "short_description"
  8785	shall be a relatively short, pithy description of the init script, where as the
  8786	"multiline_description" can be a much longer piece of text that may span
  8787	mulitple lines. In a multiline description, each continuation line must begin
  8788	with a '#' followed by tab character or a '#' followed by at least two space
  8789	characters. The multiline description is terminated by the first line that does
  8790	not match this criteria.
  8791	
  8792	The comment conventions described in this session are only required for use by
  8793	LSB-compliant applications; system init scripts as provided by LSB-compliant
  8794	run-time environments are not required to use the scheme outlined here.
  8795	
  8796	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
  8797	
  8798	Installation and removal of init.d files
  8799	
  8800	An init.d file is installed by copying it into /etc/init.d (which may be a
  8801	symlink to another location). This can be done by the package installer. During
  8802	the package's postinstall script, the program "/usr/lib/lsb/install_initd"
  8803	configures the distribution's boot script system to call the package's init.d
  8804	file at the appropriate time.
  8805	
  8806	The install_initd program takes a single argument, the pathanme to the /etc/
  8807	init.d file. For example:
  8808	
  8809	        /usr/lib/lsb/install_initd /etc/init.d/example.com-coffeed
  8810	
  8811	When a software package is removed, the package's preuninstall script shall
  8812	call /usr/lib/lsb/remove_initd and pass the pathname to the /etc/init.d file.
  8813	The package manager is still responsible for removing the /etc/init.d file; the
  8814	remove_initd program is provided in case the distribution needs to clean up any
  8815	other modifications in the distribution's boot script system that might have
  8816	been made by the install_initd program. For example:
  8817	
  8818	        /usr/lib/lsb/remove_initd /etc/init.d/example.com-coffeed
  8819	
  8820	There should be a tool available to the user (e.g., RedHat's chkconfig) which
  8821	can be used by the system administrator to easily manipulate at which init
  8822	levels a particular init.d script is started or stopped. This specification
  8823	currently does not specify such an interface, however.
  8824	
  8825	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
  8826	
  8827	Run Levels
  8828	
  8829	 The following run levels are specified for use by the "Default-Start:" and
  8830	"Default-Stop:" specifiers as defined by the section Comment conventions for
  8831	init scripts>. Many LSB run-time environments commonly use these run level
  8832	definitions, and in the absence of other considerations, providers of run-time
  8833	environments are strongly encouraged to follow this convention to provide
  8834	consistency for system administrators who need to work with multiple
  8835	distributions. However, it is not required that LSB-compliant run-time
  8836	environments use these run levels; the distribution-provided install_initd
  8837	script may map the run levels specified below to whatever
  8838	distribution-specified run levels are most appropriate. 
  8839	
  8840	0 halt                                                    
  8841	1 single user mode                                        
  8842	2 multiuser with no network services exported             
  8843	3 normal/full multiuser                                   
  8844	4 reserved for local use, default is normal/full multiuser
  8845	5 multiuser with xdm or equivalent                        
  8846	6 reboot                                                  
  8847	
  8848	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
  8849	
  8850	Facility names
  8851	
  8852	Boot facilities are used to indicate dependencies in init scripts, as defined
  8853	in a previous section. Facility names that begin with a dollar sign ('$') are
  8854	system facility names, defined by the LSB, and MUST be provided by
  8855	distributions. [5] LSB applications must not provide facilities that begin with
  8856	a dollar sign. This document defines the following facility names:
  8857	
  8858	$local_fs  all local filesystems are mounted                                   
  8859	$network   low level networking (ethernet card; may imply PCMCIA running)      
  8860	$named     daemons which may provide hostname resolution (if present) are      
  8861	           running                                                             
  8862	$portmap   daemons providing SunRPC/ONCRPC portmapping service[6] (if present) 
  8863	           are running                                                         
  8864	$remote_fs all remote filesystems are mounted[7].                              
  8865	$syslog    system logger is operational                                        
  8866	$time      the system time has been set [8]                                    
  8867	
  8868	Other (non-system) facilities may be defined by other LSB applications. These
  8869	facilities shall be named using the same conventions defined for naming init.d
  8870	script names. Commonly, the facility provided by an LSB application init.d
  8871	script will have the same name as the name assigned to the init.d script.
  8872	
  8873	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
  8874	
  8875	Script names
  8876	
  8877	Since the init.d scripts must live in a single directory, they must come from a
  8878	single namespace. Three means of assigning names from this namespace are
  8879	available:
  8880	
  8881	  * Assigned namespace. This namespace consists of names which only use the
  8882	    character set [a-z0-9]. This space is desirable for scripts which system
  8883	    administrators may often wish to run manually: e.g., "/etc/init.d/named
  8884	    restart" In order to avoid conflicts these init.d names must be reserved
  8885	    through the Linux Assigned Names and Numbers Authority (LANANA).
  8886	    Information about the LANANA may be found at www.lanana.org.
  8887	   
  8888	    Commonly used names shall be reserved in advance; developers for projects
  8889	    should be encouraged to reserve names from LANANA, so that each
  8890	    distribution can use the same name, and to avoid conflicts with other
  8891	    projects.
  8892	   
  8893	  * Hierarchical namespace. This namespace consists of scripts names which look
  8894	    like this: [hier1]-[hier2]-...-[name], where name is again taken the
  8895	    character set [a-z0-9], and where there may be one or more [hier-n]
  8896	    components. [hier1] may either be an LSB provider name assigned by the
  8897	    LANANA, or it may be owners' DNS name in lower case, with at least one '.'
  8898	    (e.g., "debian.org", "staroffice.sun.com"). The LSB provider name assigned
  8899	    by LANANA must only consist of the ASCII characters [a-z0-9].
  8900	   
  8901	  * Reserved namespace. This namespace consists of script names which begin
  8902	    with the character '_', and is reserved for distribution use only. This
  8903	    namespace should be used for core packages only, and in general use of this
  8904	    namespace is highly discouraged.  
  8905	   
  8906	In general, if a package or some system function is likely to be used on
  8907	multiple systems, the package developers or the distribution SHOULD get a
  8908	registered name through LANANA, and distributions should strive to use the same
  8909	name whenever possible. For applications which may not be "core" or may not be
  8910	commonly installed, the hierarchical namespace may be more appropriate. An
  8911	advantage to the hierarchical namespace is that there is no need to consult
  8912	with the LANANA before obtaining an assigned name.
  8913	
  8914	Short names are highly desirable, since many system administrators like to use
  8915	them to manually start and stop services. Given this, they should be
  8916	standardized on a per-package basis. This is the rationale behind having a
  8917	LANANA organization to assign these names. The LANANA may be called upon to
  8918	handle other namespace issues, such as package/prerequisites naming (which is
  8919	essential to making prerequisites to work correctly).
  8920	
  8921	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
  8922	
  8923	Init script Functions
  8924	
  8925	Each LSB-compliant init.d script must source the file /lib/lsb/init-functions.
  8926	This file must cause the following shell script commands to be defined. This
  8927	can be done either by adding a directory to the PATH variable which defines
  8928	these commands, or by defining sh aliases. While the distribution-provided
  8929	aliases may choose to use bash extensions (at the distribution's option), the
  8930	LSB init.d files themselves should only depend in /bin/sh features as defined
  8931	by POSIX.2.
  8932	
  8933	                This runs the specified program as a daemon. start_daemon will 
  8934	start_daemon    check to see if there is a program named "daemon" already      
  8935	[-f] [-n        running. If so, it will not start another copy of the daemon   
  8936	nicelevel]      unless the -f option is given. The -n option specifies a nice  
  8937	pathname [args] level. See nice(1). start_daemon should return the LSB defined 
  8938	                exit status codes. It shall return 0 if the program has been   
  8939	                successfully started or is running and not 0 otherwise.        
  8940	                This stops the specified program. The program is found using   
  8941	                the algorithm given by pidofproc. If a signal is specified,    
  8942	killproc [-p    using the -signal_name or -signal_number syntaxes specified by 
  8943	pidfile]        the kill command, the program is sent that signal. Otherwise, a
  8944	pathname        SIGTERM followed by a SIGKILL after some number of seconds is  
  8945	[signal]        sent. Compliant applications may use the basename instead of   
  8946	                the pathname. killproc should return the LSB defined exit      
  8947	                status codes. It shall return 0 if the program has been stopped
  8948	                or is not running and not 0 otherwise.                         
  8949	                This function returns one or more pid(s) for a particular      
  8950	                daemon. If an entry is found in /var/run/basename.pid, then    
  8951	                that value is returned. If the -p option is used, the given    
  8952	                pidfile is used instead. Compliant applications may use the    
  8953	                basename instead of the pathname. Compliant implementations of 
  8954	                the LSB may attempt other mechanisms for determining the pid   
  8955	pidofproc [-p   (s), although this is not required (and not recommended, since 
  8956	pidfile]        a user can trick startup scripts by creating processes that    
  8957	pathname        appear to be system programs in the process list thus creating 
  8958	                a potential security exposure). Hence, LSB-complaint           
  8959	                applications who wish to use the pidofproc function in their   
  8960	                init scripts must store the pid in /var/run/basename.pid.      
  8961	                pidofproc should return the LSB defined exist status codes for 
  8962	                "status". It shall return 0 if the program is the process is   
  8963	                running and not 0 otherwise.                                   
  8964	log_success_msg This requests the distribution to print a success message. The 
  8965	"message"       message should be relatively short; no more than 60 characters 
  8966	                is highly desirable.                                           
  8967	log_failure_msg This requests the distribution to print a failure message. The 
  8968	"message"       message should be relatively short; no more than 60 characters 
  8969	                is highly desirable.                                           
  8970	log_warning_msg This requests the distribution to print a warning message. The 
  8971	"message"       message should be relatively short; no more than 60 characters 
  8972	                is highly desirable.                                           
  8973	
  8974	Notes
  8975	
  8976	[1]  ISO/IEC 14882: 1998(E) Programming languages --C++                        
  8977	                                                                               
  8978	[2]  Linux Standard Base                                                       
  8979	                                                                               
  8980	[3]  This specification does not require, but is designed to allow, the        
  8981	     development of a system which runs boot scripts in parallel. Hence,       
  8982	     enforced-serialization of scripts is avoided unless it is explicitly      
  8983	     necessary.                                                                
  8984	                                                                               
  8985	[4]  More than one space, or a tab character, indicates the continuation line. 
  8986	                                                                               
  8987	[5]  The dollar sign does not indicate variable expansion as in many Linux     
  8988	     utilities. Starting a facility name with a dollar sign is merely a way of 
  8989	     dividing the namespace between the system and applications.               
  8990	                                                                               
  8991	[6]  as defined in RFC 1833                                                    
  8992	                                                                               
  8993	[7]  In some LSB run-time environments, filesystems such as /usr may be remote.
  8994	     Many applications that require $local_fs will probably require also       
  8995	     require $remote_fs                                                        
  8996	                                                                               
  8997	[8]  i.e., using a network-based time program such as ntp or rdate, or via the 
  8998	     hardware Real Time Clock
